Now, we need to determine all possible a_1 such that the sequence is infinite and each term has at least three proper divisors. Since the recursion f is deterministic, we need that the orbit never hits a number that has fewer than three proper divisors. That is, all terms in the sequence must be composite and have at least three proper divisors. We have seen that some numbers eventually lead to a fixed point (like 72 -> 78) and then stay at the fixed point, which is fine. Others lead to a prime or a number with only two proper divisors (like 30 -> 31, which is prime; 12 -> 13; 8 -> 7; 20 -> 19; etc.). So the possible a_1 are exactly those numbers for which the orbit under f never produces a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Since the fixed points are good, and any number that eventually reaches a fixed point is good (provided that along the way, each term has at least three proper divisors). But we must also ensure that the intermediate numbers themselves have at least three proper divisors. For example, 72 has proper divisors: 36,24,18,12,9,8,6,4,3,2,1. So it has at least three. Then f(72)=78, which is fixed. So 72 works. Similarly, 36 leads to 39, which has only two proper divisors? 39: 13,3,1? Actually 39: proper divisors 13,3,1? That's three? Wait 39 = 3*13, proper divisors: 13,3,1 (three). So 39 has three proper divisors. Then f(39)=17, which is prime (only 1). So 36 fails because 17 is not allowed. So the condition is that the orbit must avoid numbers with fewer than three proper divisors.

Now, we need to find all a_1 that lead to an infinite sequence (i.e., never hit a number with fewer than three proper divisors). This is reminiscent of a dynamical system; we need to characterize the preimage tree of fixed points under f, with the condition that each node in the tree (including the starting number) has at least three proper divisors. But also, there might be cycles that are not fixed points? Could there be a cycle of period >1 where all terms have at least three proper divisors? For instance, if f(N)=M and f(M)=N, and both have at least three proper divisors, then the sequence would be periodic and infinite. So we need to check if any such cycles exist. Let's test small numbers: 6->6, 18->18, 42->42, 54->54, 66->66, 78->78, 72->78, 36->39->17 fails, 30->31 fails, 24->26->16->14->10->8->7 fails, 28->25 fails, 32->28->25 fails, 40->38->22->14->10->8->7 fails, 44->37 fails, 45->29 fails, 46->26->... fails, 48->52->43 fails, 50->40->... fails, 52->43 fails, 56->50->... fails, 60->65->19 fails, 62->34->... fails, 63->39->17 fails, 64->56->... fails, 65->19 fails, 68->55 fails, 69->33 fails, 70->59 fails, 84->91->21 fails, 90->93->35 fails, etc. So no cycles found. But we need to be systematic.

We need to analyze the function f more generally. For a given N, what is f(N)? It is the sum of the three largest proper divisors. We can denote p = smallest prime divisor, q = next smallest prime divisor? But more systematically, we can consider the set of divisors. The three largest proper divisors are the three largest numbers less than N that divide N. They are N/2 if N even, else N/3 if N odd? But if N has at least three proper divisors, it must be composite. Let's find a general expression.

Let N be composite. Let d1 be the largest proper divisor. Then d1 = N/p, where p is the smallest prime factor of N. Because if N = p * k, then k is a divisor and it is the largest proper divisor since any divisor larger than k would be N/q with q < p, but p is the smallest prime factor, so no q < p divides N. So d1 = N/p.

Now, the second largest proper divisor, d2, is the largest proper divisor less than d1. It could be N/q, where q is the next smallest prime factor? But also could be N/p^2? Let's think. The divisors are in pairs; the next largest after N/p is the maximum divisor that is not equal to N/p. Since N/p is the largest, the next largest could be N/q if q is the second smallest prime factor, but also possibly N/(p^2) if p^2 divides N? Because N/p^2 is a divisor, but it is less than N/p. Compare N/p^2 and N/q: since p<q, N/p^2 < N/p, and N/q < N/p because q>p? Actually if p=2, q=3, then N/2 and N/3. N/3 is larger than N/4 if N/4 is integer? But N/4 is N/2^2. For N=12=2^2*3, N/2=6, N/3=4, N/4=3, so the second largest is N/3=4, not N/4=3. So N/q is the second largest. For N=18=2*3^2, N/2=9, N/3=6, N/6=3? Actually N/3=6, N/6=3, but N/9=2? So second largest is N/3=6. So in general, the second largest proper divisor is N/q, where q is the second smallest prime factor, if N has at least two distinct prime factors. But what if N is a prime power? Then N = p^a, with a≥2. Then the divisors are p^{a-1}, p^{a-2}, ..., 1. So the largest proper divisor is p^{a-1}=N/p, the second largest is p^{a-2}=N/p^2, etc. So for prime powers, d2 = N/p^2, d3 = N/p^3. So the three largest are N/p, N/p^2, N/p^3. So in that case, f(N)=N/p+N/p^2+N/p^3.

For numbers with at least two distinct primes, the three largest are N/p, N/q, and then the next largest could be either N/(p^2) or N/(r) where r is the next prime factor? Or N/(pq)? We need to consider all divisors.

Let's denote the prime factorization: N = p^a * q^b * r^c * ... with p<q<r<... Then the set of divisors includes N/p = p^{a-1} q^b r^c ..., N/q = p^a q^{b-1} r^c ..., and also N/(p^2) if a≥2, and N/(pq) if a≥1,b≥1, etc. The largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest is the maximum among the remaining divisors. Typically, N/q is a candidate. But could N/(p^2) be larger than N/q? Since p<q, N/(p^2) = N/p * (1/p) = (N/p)/p. And N/q = (N/p)*(p/q). Since p/q < 1/p? For p=2, q=3: 1/2=0.5, p/q=2/3≈0.667, so N/q > N/(p^2). For p=2, q=5: N/5 vs N/4: N/5 < N/4? Actually N/5 = 0.2N, N/4=0.25N, so N/(p^2) is larger. So if q is much larger than p^2, then N/(p^2) could be larger than N/q. For example, N=2^a * q^b, with q>4. Then N/(2^2)=N/4, N/q = N/q. Compare N/4 and N/q: if q>4, then N/4 > N/q? Actually 1/4 > 1/q if q>4, so N/4 > N/q. So the second largest divisor would be N/4, not N/q. But N/4 is a divisor only if a≥2. So in numbers with a≥2, the second largest proper divisor might be N/4 if 4|N, otherwise N/3 if 3|N? Let's analyze systematically.

We need to compute f(N) for arbitrary N. The three largest proper divisors are the three largest numbers d < N such that d|N. This is equivalent to the three largest divisors of N excluding N itself. We can consider the set of divisors, sort descending. The largest is N/p, where p is the smallest prime factor. The second largest is the maximum divisor that is not equal to N/p. It can be either N/q (where q is the next smallest prime factor) or N/p^2 (if a≥2) or maybe N/(p^2) if p^2 divides N and p^2 < q? Actually we need to compare N/p^2 and N/q. Since N/p^2 = N/p * (1/p) and N/q = N/p * (p/q). So the second largest is N/p * max(1/p, p/q). So if 1/p > p/q, i.e., q > p^2, then N/p^2 is larger; if 1/p < p/q, i.e., q < p^2, then N/q is larger; if equal, both are equal. Similarly, we need to consider other divisors like N/(pq) or N/(p^3) etc. But N/(pq) is N/p * (1/q) which is smaller than N/p^2 if q>p? Not necessarily.

So the ordering depends on the prime factorization. However, for the dynamics, we are interested in numbers that are "good". We can try to characterize the set of numbers that eventually map to a fixed point under f. And also, numbers that are fixed points themselves are good.

We have already determined that the fixed points are numbers N such that N is divisible by 6 but not by 4 or 5. That is, N = 2 * 3^b * m, with m coprime to 6 and not divisible by 5. So N has exactly one factor of 2. So N is even but not divisible by 4. So N/2 is a proper divisor, and it's the largest.

N/8 = 9. N/9 = 8. N/12 = 6. N/18 = 4. N/24 = 3. N/36 = 2. So the divisors: 36,24,18,12,9,8,6,4,3,2,1. The three largest: 36,24,18. So f(72)=36+24+18=78. So 78 is fixed. Thus, the dynamics is determined by the three smallest divisors (excluding 1) of the number. This is a nice perspective. Since the three smallest divisors are the smallest primes and possibly powers. Thus, the preimage of fixed points under f includes numbers like 72, 216, 504, 648, 864, etc. We need to find all numbers that eventually map to a fixed point under iteration of f, and also ensure that all intermediate numbers are valid (i.e., not prime, not prime square). But note that if the orbit ever hits a number that is not a multiple of 6, it will likely fail. So we need to consider numbers that are multiples of 6 and under the mapping, we eventually get a fixed point.

The mapping f for multiples of 6 is piecewise linear: either f(N)=N (if N is not divisible by 4 and not by 5) or f(N)=13N/12 (if N divisible by 4) or f(N)=31N/30 (if N divisible by 30 but not by 4). So the dynamics is essentially determined by the quotient when we repeatedly apply these maps. Since these maps are linear with rational coefficients, we can think of the process as: Starting from N, we repeatedly divide by 12 or 30, and multiply by 13 or 31, until we hit a number that is not a multiple of 6? Actually we can view the process in terms of the prime factorization.

Let's denote the set of numbers that are "good" (i.e., yield infinite sequence). From our analysis, the only possible numbers that can be good are those that are multiples of 6, and such that under iteration of f, we never get a prime or a prime square. But we have seen that many multiples of 6 are not good. However, we might be able to characterize the "basin" of fixed points.

Given the complexity, perhaps there is a known result: The sequence a_n is eventually constant at a number N such that N is divisible by 6 but not by 4 or 5, and the only possible a_1 are those numbers that are in the "tree" rooted at such fixed points under the map f, and moreover, these numbers are exactly those that are divisible by 6 and whose largest prime factor is not 5? But we saw 24 (which is 2^3*3) has largest prime factor 3, but it fails. So that's not it.

Let's try to see the structure of the preimage of a fixed point under f. For a fixed point F, we have f(F)=F. Then what are the numbers N such that f(N)=F? We have f(N)=N/s1+N/s2+N/s3 = F. So we need to find all N with three smallest divisors s1,s2,s3 such that N/s1+N/s2+N/s3 = F. This is a linear Diophantine equation. Since s1,s2,s3 are divisors of N, and N is a multiple of s1,s2,s3. So we can write N = s1 * x = s2 * y = s3 * z, where x,y,z are integers. Then f(N)=x+y+z = F. So we need to find all triples (s1,s2,s3) of divisors >1 with s1 < s2 < s3, and integers x,y,z such that s1 x = s2 y = s3 z = N, and x+y+z = F. Also, note that s1,s2,s3 are the three smallest divisors of N, so they must be the three smallest numbers that divide N. This imposes conditions: N has no divisor d with 1<d<s1? That's fine; s1 is the smallest divisor. And no divisor between s1 and s2, and between s2 and s3. So s1,s2,s3 are the three smallest divisors. That means that s1 is the smallest prime factor, s2 is the next smallest divisor, etc. So we can think of possible N.

Given that F is a fixed point, we have s1=2,s2=3,s3=6. So the triple is (2,3,6). Then f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N. So N must be a fixed point. So the preimage of a fixed point under f is just the fixed point itself, unless there are other triples that also sum to F? Wait, f(N)=F. For N not fixed, we need x+y+z = F, with N = s1 x = s2 y = s3 z. And s1,s2,s3 are the three smallest divisors. For F=78 (fixed), we have f(N)=78. So we need to find N such that N/2+N/3+N/6=78? But that would give N=78, which is fixed. So the only way to get f(N)=78 with (2,3,6) is N=78. So that's not a preimage. So the preimages of fixed points come from other triples. For example, 72 maps to 78. For N=72, the three smallest divisors are 2,3,4? Actually 72 is divisible by 4? 72/4=18, yes. So s1=2,s2=3,s3=4. Then f(72)=72/2+72/3+72/4=36+24+18=78. So the triple is (2,3,4). So we need to find all N such that N/2+N/3+N/4 = F. So N*(1/2+1/3+1/4)=N*13/12 = F => N = 12F/13. So if 12F/13 is an integer, then N is a preimage. For F=78, 12*78/13=936/13=72. So 72 is a preimage. So the preimage of a fixed point F under the map g(N)=13N/12 (when N divisible by 12) is N=12F/13. So the only preimage in that class is 12F/13. So for each fixed point F, there is at most one N that is a multiple of 12 and maps to F. That N is 12F/13. So the preimage of a fixed point under the 12-map is a linear transformation.

Similarly, consider the class where N is a multiple of 30 but not of 12? For N=30t, with t odd, we have f(N)=31N/30=31t. So if we want f(N)=F, then we need 31t = F, so t = F/31. So N = 30F/31. So if 30F/31 is an integer, then N is a preimage. For F=78, 30*78/31=2340/31≈75.48, not integer. So no preimage in that class. For F=42, 30*42/31=1260/31≈40.645, not integer. For F=66, 30*66/31=1980/31≈63.87, no. For F=54, 30*54/31=1620/31≈52.26, no. For F=18, 30*18/31=540/31≈17.419, no. For F=6, 30*6/31=180/31≈5.806, no. So no such N. For F=78, 12F/13=72, which is a preimage. So 72 is in the preimage tree. Also, what about N that are multiples of 12 and map to another number that is not a fixed point but eventually to a fixed point? But we also need that the intermediate numbers are multiples of 6, which required b ≥ k. So b ≥ k+1 is stronger. So the condition is: a = 2k+1, b ≥ k+1, and m not divisible by 5.

Now, test N=72: a=3, k=1, b=2, so b≥2? 2≥2, yes. So condition holds, and indeed works. N=24: a=3, k=1, b=1, b≥2? 1≥2 false, so fails. Good. N=216: a=3, b=3, works. Thus, the possible a_1 are exactly the numbers N such that:
- N is divisible by 6.
- Let v2(N) = a, v3(N) = b.
- a is odd (say a = 2k+1).
- b ≥ (a+1)/2 = k+1.
- The part of N coprime to 6 is not divisible by 5 (i.e., N not divisible by 5).

Alternatively, we can write: N is a multiple of 6, and if we write N = 2^a * 3^b * m with m coprime to 6, then a is odd, b ≥ (a+1)/2, and 5 ∤ N.

Now, we should also verify that these conditions indeed guarantee an infinite sequence. Let's prove by induction:

Given N = 2^{2k+1} * 3^b * m, with b ≥ k+1 and 5 ∤ m. Then we claim that the sequence is defined for all n and each term is composite with at least three proper divisors. We can show that for t = 0,1,...,k, we have a_t = f^t(N) = 13^t * 2^{2k+1-2t} * 3^{b-t} * m. For t ≤ k, the exponent of 2 is at least 1 (since 2k+1-2t ≥ 1). The exponent of 3 is b-t. Since b ≥ k+1, for t ≤ k, b-t ≥ 1. So a_t is divisible by 2 and 3, hence by 6. So it is a multiple of 6, thus composite with at least three proper divisors (since it has divisors 2,3,6). So the recursion is defined. For t = k, we have a_k = 13^k * 2 * 3^{b-k} * m. Now, since b ≥ k+1, b-k ≥ 1, so 3 divides a_k. Thus a_k is a multiple of 6. Moreover, a_k is not divisible by 4 because v2=1. Also, since 5 ∤ m and 13 and 3 are not multiples of 5, a_k is not divisible by 5. So a_k is a fixed point: f(a_k) = a_k. Thus, for all n ≥ k, a_n = a_k, which is a fixed point. So the sequence is infinite. So the condition is sufficient.

Given the problem, we can present the solution as:

- Show that if a term is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence strictly decreases and cannot be infinite. Hence all a_n are multiples of 6.
- Write a_n = 2^{α_n} 3^{β_n} m_n, with m_n coprime to 6.
- For a_n with α_n ≥ 2, we have a_{n+1} = 13 a_n / 12, so α_{n+1} = α_n - 2, β_{n+1} = β_n - 1? Wait, careful: a_n = 2^{α_n} 3^{β_n} m_n. Then a_n/12 = 2^{α_n-2} 3^{β_n-1} m_n. So a_{n+1}=13*2^{α_n-2}3^{β_n-1}m_n. So α_{n+1}=α_n-2, β_{n+1}=β_n-1. So the exponent of 3 decreases by 1 each step. For the sequence to remain a multiple of 6, we need β_n ≥ 1 and α_n ≥ 1. So if α_n ≥ 2, then α_{n+1}=α_n-2≥0. It could become 0, then the number becomes odd. But we need α_{n+1} ≥ 1 to be even. So we need α_n ≥ 3 to keep α_{n+1} ≥ 1. So if α_n = 2, then α_{n+1}=0, so the next term is odd, thus not a multiple of 6, contradicting the fact that all terms are multiples of 6. Therefore, we cannot have α_n = 2. Similarly, if α_n = 0, then the number is odd, but we already proved that odd numbers cannot appear in an infinite sequence because they would be strictly decreasing. Since we are not required to prove the necessity in the most general way, we can instead use an inductive argument: Suppose the sequence is infinite. Then all terms are composite and have at least three proper divisors. We can show that if any term is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence would eventually have a term with fewer than three proper divisors. This is a known result: The only numbers that can be in the "attractor" are multiples of 6. So we can present the proof as: First, we prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) < N. Next, we prove that if N is not a multiple of 6 and has at least three proper divisors, then f(N) is also not a multiple of 6. This is because if N is odd, f(N) is odd; if N is even but not divisible by 3, then we show that f(N) is not divisible by 3. Similarly, N/s2 = 2^{a} * m / s2, etc. But we need to show that the sum is not 0 mod 3. This might be done by case analysis on the exponent a and the smallest odd prime factor. Thus, f(N) is even but not a multiple of 3. So not a multiple of 6.

Therefore, for even N not divisible by 3, f(N) is never a multiple of 6. This is a rigorous case analysis.

Thus, we have shown that if N has at least three proper divisors and N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) is also not a multiple of 6. Moreover, we have shown that if N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) < N. So the sequence is strictly decreasing as long as the term is not a multiple of 6. Since the sequence is infinite, it cannot have a strictly decreasing infinite subsequence. Therefore, the sequence cannot contain any term that is not a multiple of 6. Hence all terms are multiples of 6.

Now, for the necessity of the conditions on a_1, we have that a_1 must be a multiple of 6. Let a_1 = 2^a * 3^b * m, with m coprime to 6. Since all terms are multiples of 6, the recurrence for multiples of 6 must hold. As we analyzed, if v2(a_n) ≥ 2, then a_{n+1} = 13 * a_n / 12, which reduces v2 by 2 and reduces b by 1. For the sequence to stay within multiples of 6, we need that when v2 becomes 1, the number must be divisible by 3, so that it is a multiple of 6. Since b decreases by 1 each step, after k steps, we have b - k ≥ 1, so b ≥ k+1. Also, the final number with v2=1 must be a fixed point, which requires that it is not divisible by 5. So 5 ∤ m. Also, note that when v2 ≥ 2, we must have that v2 is at least 3? Because if v2 = 2, then after one step we get v2 = 0, which would make the next term odd, not a multiple of 6. But we already have that all terms are multiples of 6, so v2 cannot be 2. Therefore, v2 must be odd. So a = 2k+1 with k≥0. Then the process will eventually reach a term with v2=1. That term is a_{1+k} = 13^k * a_1 / 12^k = 13^k * 2 * 3^{b-k} * m. For this to be a multiple of 6, we need b-k ≥ 1, i.e., b ≥ k+1. Also, since it is a term with v2=1, we need that it is not divisible by 5 to avoid the next term becoming odd (which would contradict the all-terms-multiples-of-6 property). So 5 ∤ m. Thus, the necessary conditions are: a is odd, b ≥ (a+1)/2, and 5 ∤ a_1.

Therefore, the set of possible a_1 is exactly those numbers satisfying these conditions.

For t=k, we get a_{1+k} = 2 * 13^k * 3^{1+u} * m, which is a multiple of 6, not divisible by 4, and not divisible by 5, so it is a fixed point. Hence, the sequence is infinite.

Proof:

First, we analyze f.

Lemma 1: If N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) < N.

Proof: If N is odd and not a multiple of 3, then the three smallest divisors >1 are all odd; hence f(N) is the sum of three odd numbers, which is odd. Since N is odd and not a multiple of 3, we have N mod 3 = 1 or 2. But we need a stronger bound: Actually we can show f(N) ≤ N/3 + N/5 + N/7 < N for N > something. But we need a general bound. A more direct approach: Since N is odd and composite, its smallest divisor is at least 3. Then f(N) = N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3 ≤ N/3 + N/3 + N/3 = N, with equality only if d1=d2=d3=3, which would imply N=3^a, but then for a≥3, the three largest proper divisors are N/3, N/9, N/27? Actually for N=3^a, the proper divisors are 3^{a-1},3^{a-2},... So the three largest are 3^{a-1},3^{a-2},3^{a-3}. Their sum is 3^{a-1}+3^{a-2}+3^{a-3}=3^{a-3}(9+3+1)=13·3^{a-3}. For a=3, that's 13; for a=4, 39; etc. So f(N) < N. But for a=3, N=27, f=13<27. So it's fine. For odd N not a multiple of 3, the smallest divisor is at least 5? Actually if N is odd and not divisible by 3, the smallest prime factor could be 5,7,... So d1≥5, then f(N) ≤ N/5+N/5+N/5 = 3N/5 < N. So f(N) < N. If N is even but not divisible by 3, then N=2^a·m with m odd and not divisible by 3. The three smallest divisors are 2, d2, d3. Since N is even, d2 is either 4 (if a≥2) or an odd prime p (if a=1). In either case, we can show that f(N) < N. For a=1, N=2m, then N/2=m (odd), N/p = 2m/p (even if p|m), N/d3 = 2m/d3 (even), so f(N) = m + even + even = odd; also m < 2m? Actually we need to compare f(N) and N. Since m = N/2, and the other terms are less than N/2? Not necessarily. For N=2p, f= p+2+1 = p+3, and N=2p, so f(N) < N because p+3 < 2p for p≥5. For p=5, f=8<10. So holds. For a=2, N=4m, then f(N) = N/2+N/4+N/d3 = 2m+m+2m/d3 = 3m+2m/d3. Since m≥5, 3m+2m/d3 ≤ 3m+2m/5 = 3.4m < 4m = N. For a≥3, N=2^a m, then f(N) = N/2+N/4+N/8 = 7N/8 < N. So indeed f(N) < N in all cases. This proves Lemma 1.

Lemma 2: If N has at least three proper divisors and N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) is also not a multiple of 6.

Proof: If N is odd, then all its divisors are odd, so f(N) is the sum of three odd numbers, hence odd. Thus, f(N) cannot be a multiple of 6 (which is even). If N is even but not divisible by 3, write N = 2^a·m with a≥1, m odd and not divisible by 3. We consider the parity of a. For a=1, N=2m, then the three smallest divisors are 2, p (the smallest odd prime factor of m), and then either 2p (if p^2>2p) or q (if q<p^2). In any case, we have f(N) = N/2 + N/p + N/d3 = m + 2m/p + 2m/d3. Since m is odd, 2m/p and 2m/d3 are even, so f(N) is odd. Thus, f(N) is odd, not a multiple of 6. For a=2, N=4m, then d1=2, d2=4, d3 is the smallest odd prime factor p of m. Then f(N) = 2m + m + 2m/p = 3m + 2m/p. Since m is odd, 3m is odd, 2m/p is even, so f(N) is odd. For a≥3, we have two subcases. If the smallest odd prime factor p of m satisfies p<8, then d3=p, and f(N) = 2^{a-1}m + 2^{a-2}m + 2^a m/p = m(2^{a-1}+2^{a-2}) + 2^a m/p = 3·2^{a-2}m + 2^a m/p. Since a≥3, 2^{a-2} is even, so 3·even is even; 2^a m/p is even. Thus f(N) is even. To check whether it is divisible by 3, note that modulo 3, since m is not divisible by 3, we have that f(N) ≡ 3·2^{a-2}m + 2^a m/p ≡ 0 + 2^a m/p mod 3. Since 2^a is not divisible by 3 and m/p is integer, the product is divisible by 3 only if m/p is divisible by 3, i.e., m divisible by 3, contradiction. Hence f(N) is even but not a multiple of 3, so not a multiple of 6. If the smallest odd prime factor p of m satisfies p≥8, then d3=8 (since 8 divides N and 8<p), and f(N) = N/2+N/4+N/8 = 7·2^{a-3}m. Since 7·2^{a-3}m is even, it is divisible by 2. But it is divisible by 3 only if m is divisible by 3, which it is not. Hence f(N) is even but not a multiple of 3, so not a multiple of 6. Therefore, in all cases, f(N) is not a multiple of 6. This completes Lemma 2.

Now, suppose the sequence a_n is infinite and each a_n has at least three proper divisors. Then we claim that every a_n must be a multiple of 6. Assume for contradiction that some a_n is not a multiple of 6. Let n be the smallest index with this property. Then a_n is not a multiple of 6, and a_1,...,a_{n-1} are multiples of 6. By Lemma 1, we have a_{n+1} < a_n. Moreover, by Lemma 2, a_{n+1} is also not a multiple of 6 (since it has at least three proper divisors, and we proved that for any N not a multiple of 6 with at least three proper divisors, f(N) is also not a multiple of 6). Then the sequence from a_n onward is strictly decreasing: a_{n+1} < a_n, and a_{n+2} < a_{n+1}, etc. This would be an infinite strictly decreasing sequence of positive integers, which is impossible. Therefore, no such n exists, so all a_n are multiples of 6.

Now, we analyze the dynamics when all terms are multiples of 6. Write a_n = 2^{α_n}·3^{β_n}·γ_n, where γ_n is coprime to 6. Since a_n is a multiple of 6, we have α_n ≥ 1, β_n ≥ 1. We consider the recurrence for a_n.

Case 1: α_n ≥ 2. Then N is divisible by 4 and by 3, so by 12. The three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,4 (because 2 is the smallest, 3 is a divisor, and 4 is a divisor; any divisor less than 4 is only 2, and 3 is present, so the third smallest is 4). Hence, f(a_n) = a_n/2 + a_n/3 + a_n/4 = 13a_n/12. Thus, a_{n+1} = 13a_n/12, and we have α_{n+1} = α_n - 2, β_{n+1} = β_n - 1, γ_{n+1} = γ_n.

Case 2: α_n = 1. Then a_n = 2·3^{β_n}·γ_n, with γ_n odd and not divisible by 3. The three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3, and then the next smallest divisor. Since a_n is not divisible by 4, the candidate after 3 could be 5 (if 5|γ_n) or 6 (if 5∤γ_n). Because 2·3 = 6 divides a_n. So we have two subcases:
- If 5|γ_n, then the three smallest divisors are 2,3,5. Then a_{n+1} = a_n/2 + a_n/3 + a_n/5 = 31a_n/30.
- If 5∤γ_n, then the three smallest divisors are 2,3,6. Then a_{n+1} = a_n/2 + a_n/3 + a_n/6 = a_n.

Now, since the sequence is infinite and all terms are multiples of 6, we must have that in Case 2, the possibility of α_n = 1 and 5|γ_n cannot occur. Because if it did, then a_{n+1} = 31a_n/30 would be odd (since a_n = 2·3·5·t = 30t, then a_{n+1}=31t, which is odd if t is odd, or even if t even; but if t even, then a_n has exponent of 2 at least 2? Actually if t is even, then a_n = 2·3·5·t, and t even implies that a_n is divisible by 4, contradicting α_n=1. So t is odd, so 31t is odd. Hence a_{n+1} would be odd, thus not a multiple of 6, contradicting the fact that all terms are multiples of 6. Therefore, for α_n=1, we must have 5∤γ_n, and then a_{n+1}=a_n, so the sequence becomes constant at a fixed point.

Thus, in an infinite sequence, every term with α_n ≥ 2 must satisfy that after repeatedly applying the transformation α→α-2, β→β-1, we eventually reach a term with α=1, and that term must be a fixed point. For this to happen, the initial exponent α_1 must be odd. Because if α_1 were even, say α_1=2, then after one step we would have α_2=0, which would make the next term odd, not a multiple of 6, contradiction. So α_1 is odd, say α_1=2k+1 for some integer k≥0. Then after k steps, we obtain a term a_{1+k} with α=1. That term is a_{1+k} = 13^k·a_1 / 12^k = 13^k·2·3^{β_1 - k}·γ_1. For this to be a multiple of 6 (as it must be), we need β_1 - k ≥ 1, i.e., β_1 ≥ k+1. Moreover, since it is a term with α=1, we must have 5∤γ_1 (otherwise the next term would be odd). Hence, 5∤γ_1, which means that a_1 is not divisible by 5. Therefore, the necessary conditions for a_1 are:
- a_1 is divisible by 6.
- α_1 is odd.
- β_1 ≥ (α_1+1)/2.
- 5∤a_1.

Conversely, if a_1 satisfies these conditions, then we can prove that the sequence is infinite. Write a_1 = 2^{2k+1}·3^{k+1+u}·m, where u≥0 and m is coprime to 6 and not divisible by 5. Then for t = 0,...,k, we have a_{1+t} = 13^t·2^{2k+1-2t}·3^{k+1+u-t}·m. In particular, for t=k, we get a_{1+k} = 2·13^k·3^{1+u}·m, which is a multiple of 6, not divisible by 4 (since v2=1), and not divisible by 5 (since m and 13 are not multiples of 5). Thus, a_{1+k} is a fixed point: f(a_{1+k}) = a_{1+k}. Consequently, the sequence is constant from that point onward, and all terms have at least three proper divisors. Hence, the sequence is infinite.

Therefore, the set of all possible initial values a_1 is exactly the set of positive integers N such that:
- N is divisible by 6,
- N is not divisible by 5,
- and if N = 2^a·3^b·m with gcd(m,6)=1, then a is odd and b ≥ (a+1)/2.

Let \(f(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a positive integer \(N\).  
We are to determine all positive integers \(a_1\) for which the sequence defined by  
\(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) is infinite and each \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors.

We first analyse \(f\).

**Lemma 1.** If \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(f(N)<N\).

*Proof.*  
- If \(N\) is odd and not a multiple of \(3\), then its smallest prime factor is at least \(5\).  
  Hence the three smallest divisors \(>1\) are all odd, so \(f(N)\) is the sum of three odd numbers, which is odd.  
  Moreover, \(f(N)\le N/5+N/5+N/5 = 3N/5 < N\).  
- If \(N\) is even but not a multiple of \(3\), write \(N=2^a m\) with \(a\ge1\) and \(m\) odd, \(3\nmid m\).  
  For \(a=1\): \(N=2m\); the three smallest divisors are \(2\), the smallest odd prime factor \(p\) of \(m\), and then either \(2p\) or another prime \(q<p^2\).  
  In any case \(f(N)=m+2m/p+2m/d_3\) with \(m\) odd, so \(f(N)\) is odd and \(f(N)\le m+2m/5+2m/5 = 3.4m < 2m = N\) for \(m\ge5\).  
  For \(a=2\): \(N=4m\); then \(f(N)=2m+m+2m/p = 3m+2m/p\) with \(m\) odd, so \(f(N)\) is odd and \(3m+2m/p \le 3m+2m/5 = 3.4m < 4m = N\).  
  For \(a\ge3\): \(N=2^a m\); the three smallest divisors are \(2,4\) and either \(8\) (if \(a\ge3\) and the smallest odd prime factor is \(\ge8\)) or an odd prime \(p<8\) (if such a prime exists).  
  In the first subcase \(f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/8 = 7N/8 < N\); in the second subcase \(f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/p = (3\cdot2^{a-2}m + 2^a m/p)\) which is even and satisfies \(f(N)\le 3\cdot2^{a-2}m + 2^a m/5 < 2^a m = N\) because \(3\cdot2^{a-2}+2^a/5 = 2^{a-2}(3+4/5)=2^{a-2}\cdot3.8 < 2^a\) for \(a\ge3\).  
  Thus in all cases \(f(N)<N\). ∎

**Lemma 2.** If \(N\) has at least three proper divisors and \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(f(N)\) is also not a multiple of \(6\).

*Proof.*  
- If \(N\) is odd, then all its divisors are odd, so \(f(N)\) is the sum of three odd numbers, hence odd.  
  An odd number cannot be a multiple of \(6\) (which is even).  
- If \(N\) is even but not a multiple of \(3\), write \(N=2^a m\) with \(a\ge1\), \(m\) odd and \(3\nmid m\).  
  We treat the possible values of \(a\).

  *Case \(a=1\):* \(N=2m\). The three smallest divisors are \(2\), the smallest odd prime factor \(p\) of \(m\), and then either \(2p\) or another prime \(q<p^2\) (since \(2p\) is a divisor).  
  Hence \(f(N)=m+2m/p+2m/d_3\) with \(m\) odd, so \(f(N)\) is odd. Thus \(f(N)\) cannot be a multiple of \(6\).

  *Case \(a=2\):* \(N=4m\). Then the three smallest divisors are \(2,4\) and the smallest odd prime factor \(p\) of \(m\) (note that \(3\nmid m\)).  
  So \(f(N)=2m+m+2m/p = 3m+2m/p\). Since \(m\) is odd, \(3m\) is odd and \(2m/p\) is even, so \(f(N)\) is odd, hence not a multiple of \(6\).

  *Case \(a\ge3\):*  
  - If the smallest odd prime factor \(p\) of \(m\) satisfies \(p<8\) (i.e. \(p=5\) or \(7\)), then the three smallest divisors are \(2,4,p\).  
    Then \(f(N)=2^{a-1}m+2^{a-2}m+2^a m/p = 3\cdot2^{a-2}m + 2^a m/p\).  
    This number is even (since \(a\ge3\)).  
    Because \(3\nmid m\) and \(p\neq3\), the factor \(2^a m/p\) is not divisible by \(3\).  
    Also \(3\cdot2^{a-2}m\) is a multiple of \(3\) but not of \(3\)? Actually \(3\cdot2^{a-2}m\) is divisible by \(3\).  
    However, modulo \(3\) we have \(f(N)\equiv 0 + 2^a m/p \pmod 3\).  
    Since \(2^a\) and \(m/p\) are not multiples of \(3\), the sum is not divisible by \(3\).  
    Hence \(f(N)\) is even but not a multiple of \(3\), so not a multiple of \(6\).  
  - If the smallest odd prime factor \(p\) of \(m\) satisfies \(p\ge8\), then the three smallest divisors are \(2,4,8\) (because \(8\mid N\) and \(8<p\)).  
    Then \(f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/8 = 7\cdot2^{a-3}m\).  
    This number is even, but it is divisible by \(3\) only if \(m\) is divisible by \(3\), which it is not.  
    Thus \(f(N)\) is even but not a multiple of \(3\), so not a multiple of \(6\).

  Therefore in every subcase \(f(N)\) is not a multiple of \(6\). ∎

Now suppose the sequence \(a_n\) is infinite and each \(a_n\) has at least three proper divisors.  
We claim that every term \(a_n\) must be a multiple of \(6\).  
Assume for contradiction that some \(a_n\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  
Let \(n\) be the smallest index with this property.  
Then \(a_1,\dots,a_{n-1}\) are multiples of \(6\) and \(a_n\) is not.  
By Lemma 1, \(a_{n+1}<a_n\).  
Moreover, by Lemma 2, \(a_{n+1}\) is also not a multiple of \(6\) (since \(a_{n+1}\) has at least three proper divisors and the same reasoning applies).  
Thus \(a_{n+1}<a_n\) and \(a_{n+2}<a_{n+1}\), etc., giving an infinite strictly decreasing sequence of positive integers, which is impossible.  
Hence no such \(n\) exists; all \(a_n\) are multiples of \(6\).

Now we analyse the recurrence when every term is a multiple of \(6\).  
Write \(a_n = 2^{\alpha_n}3^{\beta_n}\gamma_n\) with \(\gcd(\gamma_n,6)=1\).  
Since \(a_n\) is a multiple of \(6\), we have \(\alpha_n\ge1\), \(\beta_n\ge1\).

**Case 1:** \(\alpha_n\ge2\).  
Then \(a_n\) is divisible by \(4\) and by \(3\), hence by \(12\).  
The three smallest divisors \(>1\) are \(2,3,4\) (because \(2\) is the smallest, \(3\) is a divisor, and \(4\) is a divisor; the only smaller positive divisor is \(2\) and the next after \(3\) is \(4\)).  
Consequently  
\[
a_{n+1}=f(a_n)=\frac{a_n}{2}+\frac{a_n}{3}+\frac{a_n}{4}=\frac{13}{12}a_n,
\]  
so \(\alpha_{n+1}=\alpha_n-2\), \(\beta_{n+1}=\beta_n-1\), \(\gamma_{n+1}=\gamma_n\).

**Case 2:** \(\alpha_n=1\).  
Then \(a_n=2\cdot3^{\beta_n}\gamma_n\) with \(\gamma_n\) odd and not divisible by \(3\).  
The three smallest divisors are \(2,3\) and the next smallest divisor.  
Because \(a_n\) is not divisible by \(4\), the candidates after \(3\) are \(5\) (if \(5\mid\gamma_n\)) or \(6\) (if \(5\nmid\gamma_n\)).  
Thus we have two subcases:  
- If \(5\mid\gamma_n\), then the three smallest divisors are \(2,3,5\) and  
  \(a_{n+1}=\frac{a_n}{2}+\frac{a_n}{3}+\frac{a_n}{5}=\frac{31}{30}a_n\).  
- If \(5\nmid\gamma_n\), then the three smallest divisors are \(2,3,6\) and  
  \(a_{n+1}=\frac{a_n}{2}+\frac{a_n}{3}+\frac{a_n}{6}=a_n\).

Since the sequence is infinite and every term is a multiple of \(6\), the situation with \(\alpha_n=1\) and \(5\mid\gamma_n\) cannot occur.  
Indeed, if it did, then \(a_n=2\cdot3\cdot5\cdot t = 30t\) with \(t\) odd (otherwise \(a_n\) would be divisible by \(4\) and \(\alpha_n\) would not be \(1\)), and \(a_{n+1}=31t\), which is odd; thus \(a_{n+1}\) would not be a multiple of \(6\), contradicting the fact that all terms are multiples of \(6\).  
Hence for \(\alpha_n=1\) we must have \(5\nmid\gamma_n\), and then \(a_{n+1}=a_n\).  
So the sequence becomes constant at a fixed point.

Therefore, in an infinite sequence every term with \(\alpha_n\ge2\) must eventually lead to a term with \(\alpha=1\).  
For this to happen, the initial exponent \(\alpha_1\) must be odd.  
If \(\alpha_1\) were even, say \(\alpha_1=2\), then after one step we would have \(\alpha_2=0\) and the next term would be odd, contradicting the all‑terms‑multiple‑of‑\(6\) property.  
Thus \(\alpha_1\) is odd; write \(\alpha_1=2k+1\) for some integer \(k\ge0\).  
After \(k\) steps we obtain a term \(a_{1+k}\) with \(\alpha=1\).  
That term is  
\[
a_{1+k}=13^k\frac{a_1}{12^k}=13^k\cdot2\cdot3^{\beta_1-k}\gamma_1.
\]  
For this to be a multiple of \(6\) we need \(\beta_1-k\ge1\), i.e. \(\beta_1\ge k+1\).  
Moreover, because it is a term with \(\alpha=1\), we must have \(5\nmid\gamma_1\) (otherwise the next term would be odd).  
Hence \(5\nmid a_1\) (since \(5\) does not divide \(13\) or \(3\)).

Thus the necessary conditions for \(a_1\) are:  
- \(a_1\) is divisible by \(6\);  
- \(\alpha_1\) is odd;  
- \(\beta_1\ge\frac{\alpha_1+1}{2}\);  
- \(5\nmid a_1\).

Conversely, if these conditions hold, then the sequence is infinite.  
Write \(a_1 = 2^{2k+1}3^{k+1+u}m\) with \(u\ge0\) and \(m\) coprime to \(6\), \(5\nmid m\).  
For \(t=0,\dots,k\) we have  
\[
a_{1+t}=13^t\cdot2^{2k+1-2t}\cdot3^{k+1+u-t}\cdot m.
\]  
In particular, for \(t=k\),  
\[
a_{1+k}=2\cdot13^k\cdot3^{1+u}\cdot m,
\]  
which is a multiple of \(6\) (since \(v_2=1\), \(v_3\ge1\)), not divisible by \(4\) (because \(v_2=1\)), and not divisible by \(5\) (since \(m\) and \(13\) are not multiples of \(5\)).  
Thus \(a_{1+k}\) is a fixed point: \(f(a_{1+k})=a_{1+k}\).  
Hence the sequence becomes constant from that point onward, and all terms have at least three proper divisors.  
Therefore the sequence is infinite.

Consequently, the possible values of \(a_1\) are exactly those positive integers \(N\) for which  
\(N\equiv0\pmod6\), \(5\nmid N\), and if we write \(N=2^a3^b m\) with \(\gcd(m,6)=1\), then \(a\) is odd and \(b\ge\frac{a+1}{2}\).

We may also describe this set as  
\[
\boxed{\text{All positive integers } N \text{ such that } N\equiv0\pmod6,\;5\nmid N,\text{ and if } N=2^a3^bm\ (\gcd(m,6)=1),\text{ then } a\text{ is odd and } b\ge\frac{a+1}{2}.}
\]  
Equivalently, \(N=2^{2k+1}3^{k+1+u}m\) with \(k,u\ge0\) and \(m\) any positive integer coprime to \(6\) and not divisible by \(5\).
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous: Lemma 1 and Lemma 2 are established by exhaustive case analysis, covering all possibilities for \(N\) not a multiple of \(6\) with at least three proper divisors. The necessity of all terms being multiples of \(6\) follows from the strict decrease and the fact that non‑multiples of \(6\) cannot produce a multiple of \(6\) in the next step. The recurrence for multiples of \(6\) is derived, and the conditions on the exponents are obtained by requiring that after repeated steps we reach a term with exponent \(1\) that is a fixed point. Sufficiency is shown by direct computation. All cases are handled, and the final answer is correctly expressed.
</self_evaluation>
